href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cobalt+Metaphosphate&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cobalt%20metaphosphate">cobalt metaphosphate</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Selective+Phase+Formation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_selective%20phase%20formation">selective phase formation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-+And+Potassium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-%20and%20potassium-ion%20batteries">sodium- and potassium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Performance&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20performance">high performance</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the development of energy storage systems, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) are emerging as promising alternatives for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), especially for large-scale applications to alleviate the drawbacks of LIBs caused by the huge demands on the uneven-distributed and limited lithium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> However, much larger ionic radii (Na<sup>+</sup>: 1.02 Å, K<sup>+</sup>: 1.38 Å) together with higher molecular weights (Na: 23, K: 39) than lithium inevitably result in slower reaction kinetics and lower reversible capacity for SIBs and PIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 and 4">(1,4)</a> The development of suitable and novel nanomaterials to achieve fast and durable Na/K-ion storage is a critical issue and still remains a challenge.</div><div class="NLM_p">Up to now, various nanomaterials have been developed for battery systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 and 5">(1,5)</a> Nevertheless, the commercial LIB anode, graphite, only could achieve an ultra-low capacity of 35 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a limited capacity of 279 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in SIBs and PIBs, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> Expanding the interlayer distancing could enhance the reversible capacity and improve the cyclability of graphite to some extent, but it is still far from practical applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> Although alloy-type (metallic and intermetallic materials) and conversion-type materials (metal phosphides/sulfides/oxides) exhibited high theoretical capacity, most reported materials suffered from large material pulverization and fast capacity fading caused by the huge volume expansion during repeated sodium/potassium ion intercalation/deintercalation process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9−11)</a> In addition, organic materials have demonstrated the capability of storing sodium/potassium ions, but the intrinsic low electronic conductivity and poor reaction reversibility still remain a big challenge that impedes further applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12,13)</a> Various strategies, including delicate nanostructure design to facilitate reaction kinetics and coating or heteroatom doping to enhance electronic conductivity, have been developed to overcome these drawbacks. In spite of some encouraging success, the underlying limitations of the above-mentioned materials have not been fundamentally solved.</div><div class="NLM_p">As a type of new emerging materials, transition-metal phosphates (Me-PO<sub><i>x</i></sub>), including pyrophosphates (Co<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> phosphates [CoHPO<sub>4</sub>·3H<sub>2</sub>O, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Mn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> and VOPO<sub>4</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a>], and mixed phosphates [Na<sub>7</sub>(MP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>4</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (M = Al, Cr, Fe)], <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> have attracted broad attention in batteries, supercapacitors, and catalysis owing to their unique physical/chemical characteristics. Pursuing phosphate chemistry further, metaphosphate (Me-PO<sub>3</sub>) also developed for various applications. For example, Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were fabricated through a thermolytic molecular precursor (TMP) method and utilized as a high-performance electrocatalyst for water oxidation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> The self-supported Ni(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> electrode with outstanding capacitive behavior was also reported. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> However, the synthesis of metaphosphate generally needs a long reaction time, the addition of coordinating reagents/surfactants, or the preparation of complex precursors. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Moreover, relevant research related to the sodium/potassium ion storage performance of metaphosphate has never been reported. It is of significant importance to explore the facile synthesis method for metaphosphate with a well-designed morphology and specified crystallinity as well as to investigate the potential sodium/potassium ion storage properties.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, cobalt metaphosphate nanosheet arrays self-supported on carbon cloths [Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC] were fabricated through a hydrothermal method combined with a phosphating process and used as an anode material for SIBs and PIBs for the first time. Unlike the traditional TMP method, our work avoided the complex preparation process of the {Co[O<sub>2</sub>P(O<sup><i>t</i></sup>Bu)<sub>2</sub>]<sub>2</sub>}<sub><i>n</i></sub> precursor, achieving Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanosheet arrays full of nanopores vertically growing on the surface of CC, which ensures sufficient electrolyte infiltration and fast electron transport among the electrodes. Phase formation analysis revealed that the activated CC helps suppress PH<sub>3</sub>-involved reaction and promotes the HPO<sub>3</sub>-involved reaction during the phosphating process, resulting in the formation of the crystalline Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> phase. When used as an anode for SIBs, the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode demonstrated a reversible multi-step sodiation/desodiation reaction with a high reversible capacity of 667 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, along with almost no capacity fading over 500 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. It also exhibited high reversible capacity in PIBs, demonstrating that metaphosphate nanomaterials are novel and promising Na/K-ion storage materials.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The synthesis process of the self-supported Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode is systematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. Before reacting, the conductive commercial CC was pre-treated by KMnO<sub>4</sub> solution and then concentrated nitric acid solution to create oxygen functional groups for nucleation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> A hydrothermal reaction was conducted to achieve the Co precursor with a uniform nanosheet array morphology on the surface of CC. During the phosphating process, the Co precursor was transferred to Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanosheets after reacting with the phosphides from the decomposition of NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> at around 300 °C. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images for the Co precursor are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. Clearly, each carbon fiber is fully covered by densely packed interwoven nanosheet arrays. The magnified SEM image further reveals that these nanosheets are vertically growing and mutually cross-linked on the surface of CC with a width of hundreds of nanometers and a thickness of tens of nanometers. After phosphating, the vertical nanosheet morphology is inherited, but the coverage decreases, and the sheet becomes thinner (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b,c).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/medium/am1c08950_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/large/am1c08950_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration of the self-supported Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode. (b,c) SEM images, (d,e) TEM images, (f) HRTEM image (the inset is the SAED pattern), and (g–j) HAADF and element mapping images for the self-supported Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/large/am1c08950_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08950&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The representative transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of the Co precursor further verify the nanosheet morphology with a smooth surface (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). Interestingly, the surface of the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanosheet is rough and full with nanopores of about 2–5 nm, which may be originated from the erosion effect of PH<sub>3</sub> gas during the phosphating reaction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d). Those thin nanosheets with abundant pores could give rise to shortened ion-transport pathways and efficient infiltration of electrolytes, rendering accelerated diffusion kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Moreover, the porous morphology would give rise to more exposed active sites for Na/K ion storage, contributing to higher reversible capacity for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> shows an interlayer distance of 0.61 nm, corresponding to the (011) plane of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>. As displayed in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f, the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) result shows continuous circles, demonstrating polycrystalline characteristics of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>. High-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) and elemental mapping images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g–j) and the corresponding energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>) further confirm the existence and homogeneous distributions of the Co, P, and O elements among the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanosheets.</div><div class="NLM_p">The phase transformation process during the synthesis of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> was evaluated through X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, the two broad peaks at about 25 and 43° are assigned to the (002) and (101) planes of carbon, which is originated from the CC. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Besides, all the diffraction peaks for the Co precursor can be well assigned to cobalt nitrate hydroxide [Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub>, JCPDS no. 46-0605], indicating that the Co precursor is mainly Co<sup>2+</sup>. After the phosphating reaction, the diffraction peaks from CC are well maintained. Meanwhile, all the other diffraction peaks can be well indexed to the monoclinic Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> [JCPDS card no. 27-1120], demonstrating that the Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub> precursor has successfully converted to Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> after phosphating.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/medium/am1c08950_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/large/am1c08950_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD pattern for Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub> NSs/CC and Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrodes. (b) Co 2p, (c) P 2p, and (d) O 1s XPS spectra for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/large/am1c08950_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08950&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The formation mechanism for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> was systematically investigated by comparing the phase structure of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC with the sample obtained from Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub> powder without the assistance of CC. Notably, when phosphating Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub> powder with NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> at 300 °C under the same experimental condition, crystalline CoP [JCPDS card no. 29-0497] phase is achieved instead of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). From previous reports, the decomposition reaction of NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> is shown as follows (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>): <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/medium/am1c08950_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i4"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">Clearly, the decomposition of NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> not only produces PH<sub>3</sub> gas but also generates HPO<sub>3</sub>. Accordingly, the Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub> precursor participates in two competitive reactions, which would be favorable for the formation of CoP and Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, respectively. During the phosphating process, the PH<sub>3</sub>-involved reaction is suppressed, and the HPO<sub>3</sub>-involved reaction becomes dominant after the assistance of CC, resulting in the formation of the final Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> From the above discussion, the synthesis mechanism of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> can be summarized as <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/medium/am1c08950_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">The chemical states of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC were characterized by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). Peaks of the Co, P, O, and C elements are found in the survey spectrum, supporting the pure phase property of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC. As seen, Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> exhibits a completely different Co 2p spectrum in comparison with CoP powder. In the spectrum of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, the two spin–orbit doublets at 782.4 and 798.4 eV can be assigned to Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, and the other two peaks at 786.7 and 803.6 eV are the shakeup satellites for Co 2p (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> However, the main peaks for CoP belong to Co<sup>3+</sup> species (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> At the P 2p spectrum, Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> shows a P–O peak at 134.5 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c); however, additional Co–P peaks appear in CoP, further indicating different crystal structures of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC and CoP (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> The O 1s spectrum of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d) exhibits two different forms, including Co–O at 531.8 eV and P–O at 533.3 eV, further confirming the successful synthesis of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The sodium ion storage performance of the novel Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode was explored in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. Before testing, the sodium ion storage performance for the pure CC was tested to exclude its capacity contribution, which shows negligible capacity at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. For the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode, the plateau at around 1.0 V during the initial discharge process at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> can be ascribed from the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film and the side reactions between the electrode and electrolyte (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> With further discharging to a lower potential, a long plateau appears below 0.25 V, which is originated from the initial sodiation reaction. At the subsequent cycle, two pairs of charge–discharge plateaus show up in the curves, indicating a multi-step reaction process. Notably, the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode shows negligible capacity fading with a high reversible capacity of 612 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> maintained after 50 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). The charge–discharge curves after different cycles overlap, and the plateaus are well preserved, suggesting high reversibility and excellent cycling stability for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/medium/am1c08950_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/large/am1c08950_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Sodium ion storage performance of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC: (a) initial discharge–charge profiles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Cycling performance at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Charge–discharge curves at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after different cycles. (d) Rate capability at different current densities. (e) Long-term cycling performance for at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> over 500 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/large/am1c08950_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08950&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The superiority of the novel Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode was further verified by its rate performance. Reversible capacities of 667 and 303 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> are achieved at 50 and 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Even at 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, a high reversible capacity of 132 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> could still be maintained. When it cycles back to 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, a capacity equals to the original value can be recovered. Small polarization at the charge–discharge curves as the current density increases demonstrates high reversibility and good rate capability of the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The long-term cycling stability of the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode was investigated at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> over 500 cycles. Before long-term cycling, the electrode was activated at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for five cycles. To our surprise, the electrode shows almost no capacity fading during cycling, and a high reversible capacity of 428 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> is achieved after 500 cycles. It should be noted that the reversible capacity and cycling stability for the novel Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC are much better than those of previously reported metal oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> metal sulfides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> and metal phosphides, such as GeP nanosheets, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Cu<sub>3</sub>P nanowires, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> self-healing SnP<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> cobalt-doped FeS<sub>2</sub> nanospheres, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> and so forth (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). As is well known, inferior cyclability for most transition-metal oxides/sulfides/phosphides is the main factor that limits their practical applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref30" aria-label="reference 9 and 30">(9,30)</a> In this work, the novel Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC anode shows excellent cycling stability even without any coating, carbon modification, and specific structure design, suggesting the great practical application value of the novel material. To investigate the structural stability of the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/CC anode, we tested the morphology of the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode after 100 cycles. As can be seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, although the nanosheet array structure has been damaged to a certain extent, the nanosheets are still visible and well support on the surface of CC after charging–discharging at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 100 cycles, which may be one of the main reasons for the excellent cycling stability for the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode.</div><div class="NLM_p">The sodium ion storage mechanism and kinetics of the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode were evaluated through conducting cyclic voltammetry (CV) at different scan rates between 0.01 and 3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). The CV curve at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> shows an irreversible broad peak at about 0.75 V, which is from the formation of SEI and side reaction of the electrode and electrolyte, well consistent with the charge–discharge results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> After the first sodiation, two oxidation peaks appear at 0.4 and 2.0 V, and the corresponding reduction peaks are located at 0.18 and 1.2 V, respectively, demonstrating a multi-step sodiation/desodiation process. Clearly, the CV curves at the initial three cycles overlap well, reflecting the high reversibility of the sodium ion storage reactions. As the scan rate increases from 0.2 to 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the position of both oxidation and reduction peaks remains unchanged (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). Even if the scan rates increase to 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the shift of peak position is still very low, demonstrating low polarization of the electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/medium/am1c08950_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/large/am1c08950_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC (a) at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, (b) at the scan range of 0.2–1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and (c) at the scan range of 2.0–10.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). (d) Determined <i>b</i>-value for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC calculated through the relationship between peak current and scan rate. (e) Normalized contribution proportions of capacitance and diffusion at 4 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC. (f) Normalized contribution proportions of capacitance and diffusion for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC at different scan rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/large/am1c08950_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08950&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The sodium ion storage mechanism was also assessed through calculating the relationship between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rates (<i>v</i>) (the detailed calculation process is presented in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, eq S1). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, a <i>b</i>-value of 0.84 is calculated for the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode, indicating that both ion diffusion-controlled charges and capacitive charges contribute to the sodium ion storage capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> The capacitive contributions for the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode at different scan rates are calculated based on <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">eq S2</a>, and a high ratio of 61.7% is obtained at 4 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e). At 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the capacitive contribution ratio goes up to 81.9% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f). A high ratio of the capacitive contribution could give rise to rapid sodium ion storage, which may be one of the main reasons for the excellent rate capability of the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The potassium ion storage behaviors of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrodes were also investigated by CV. In the initial anodic scan, the two reduction peaks located at 0.4 and 0.1 V can be assigned to the formation of the SEI film and the initial potassiation reaction, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). In the subsequent cycles, a pair of oxidation/reduction peaks are well preserved at 0.78/0.15 V with high reversibility, indicating that Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> could reversibly store potassium ions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). Charge–discharge curves for the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the initial two cycles are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. It exhibits an initial discharge–charge capacity of 1175/671 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, demonstrating an initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 57%. It should be noted that the low coulombic efficiency caused by the conversion reaction mechanism of metal oxides/sulfides/phosphides is the main obstacle that limits their practical applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> For the novel Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode, the ICE should be further promoted by enhancing the initial reversibility of the potassium ion storage reaction. Notably, the discharge plateau for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC is as low as 0.1 V, which is favorable for achieving high-energy-density PIBs. As seen, Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC shows excellent cycling stability at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> over 50 cycles, and a reversible capacity of 588 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/medium/am1c08950_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/large/am1c08950_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Potassium storage performance of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC: (a) cyclic voltammograms for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0–1.5 V (vs K<sup>+</sup>/K). (b) Initial discharge–charge profiles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycling performance at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate capability at different current densities. (e) Comparison of rate capability for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC with those of previously reported anodes for potassium storage. (f) Long-term cycling performance at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> over 150 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/asset/images/large/am1c08950_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08950&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The rate capability for the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode was evaluated. As the current density increases from 50, 200, 500, and 1000 to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, reversible capacities of 678, 629, 503, 355, and 189 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> are achieved, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). When the current density returns to 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after the rate test, a high reversible capacity of 633 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> could still be recovered. It should be noted that Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC exhibits much better rate capability in comparison with previously reported carbon-based materials (graphite, hard carbon, etc.), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> MXene-related materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> titanium base materials, and metal phosphides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> further confirming its high practical value (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e). The long-term cyclability result for the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode shows an obvious capacity increase, and the highest reversible capacity of 667 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained in the initial several cycles, which can be ascribed to the activation process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f). In the subsequent cycles, the capacity gradually decreases, and a reversible capacity of 379 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> is retained after 150 cycles. According to previous works, the long-term cycling stability for PIBs is generally worse than that of SIBs owing to a much larger potassium-ion radius and a higher atomic mass. In this work, the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrode exhibits excellent cycling stability in SIBs; however, the cyclability in PIBs needs to be further promoted in our future works through carbon coating or nanostructure modification.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">A novel high-performance sodium/potassium ion storage anode based on cobalt metaphosphate nanosheet arrays self-supported on CC [Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC] was developed via a hydrothermal method combined with a phosphating process. By adopting the active CC as a substrate, the PH<sub>3</sub>-involved reaction was suppressed, and HPO<sub>3</sub>-involved reaction became dominant during the phosphating process, consequently resulting in the formation of the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> phase instead of CoP. This synthesis strategy is of high efficiency and avoids the preparation of the complex {Co[O<sub>2</sub>P(O<sup><i>t</i></sup>Bu)<sub>2</sub>]<sub>2</sub>}<sub><i>n</i></sub> precursor, which significantly simplifies the synthesis process compared to previous works. In this electrode, the porous Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanosheet arrays are vertically growing and mutually cross-linked on the surface of the activated CC, ensuring sufficient electrolyte infiltration and fast electron transport among the electrodes. When first used as an anode in SIBs, it showed a multi-step sodium storage process with high reversibility. A high reversible capacity of 667 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was obtained at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> along with excellent cycling stability (428 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with almost no capacity decay over 500 cycles). The potassium-ion storage analysis for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC demonstrated a high reversible capacity of 588 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, outperforming most previously reported anodes in PIBs. This work highlights the promising practical application of the novel Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanomaterial and advances SIBs and PIBs one step closer toward real-world applications.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All chemical reagents, including cobalt(II) nitrate hexahydrate, ammonium chloride, sodium hypophosphite (NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub>), and ethanol, were purchased from Sinopharm Co., Ltd. All the raw materials were used directly without any further purification. CC was purchased from CeTech, Taiwan.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Synthesis of Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub> Nanosheet Arrays Self-Supported on CC [Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub>/CC]</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Before reacting, CC was cut into pieces with a size of about 1 cm × 1 cm, then immersed in aqueous KMnO<sub>4</sub> solution (5 wt %) and concentrated nitric acid for 2 and 12 h, respectively, and then rinsed with deionized water and ethanol. After drying at 80 °C in a vacuum oven for 8 h, the activated CC was collected for the next procedure. In a typical synthesis, Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (2 mmol) and NH<sub>4</sub>Cl (2 mmol) were dissolved in deionized water (50 mL) and transferred to a Teflon-lined stainless autoclave. Then, several pieces of activated CC (1 cm × 1 cm) were immersed into the above solution and reacted at 120 °C for 12 h. After rinsing with deionized water and drying at 80 °C in a vacuum oven for 12 h, the final Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub> NSs/CC precursor was obtained. For comparison, Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub> powder was prepared by the same method but without adding CC in the hydrothermal reaction.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Synthesis of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> Nanosheet Arrays Self-Supported on CC [Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC]</h3><div class="NLM_p last">First, Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub>/CC and NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> were placed in two porcelain boats separately and both of them were placed in a tube furnace. These samples were heated to 250 °C at a heating rate of 5 °C/min and then to 300 °C at 1 °C/min and kept at 300 °C for 2 h in an Ar atmosphere. After naturally cooling down to room temperature with the protection of an Ar atmosphere, the Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC electrodes were achieved. The mass loading of Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanosheets on each CC was about 1.5–2.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. For comparison, CoP was prepared through phosphating Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub> powder under the same conditions.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Powder XRD data were obtained on an X-ray diffractometer (DX-2700, Dandong Haoyuan) using a Cu Kα<sub>1</sub> source (λ = 1.5418 Å) operated at 40 kV. XPS was performed using an ESCALAB 250Xi spectrometer. The morphology of all samples was characterized by SEM (Helios Navo NanoSEM230), TEM, and HRTEM (Titan G2 60-300).</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC was utilized as the working electrode directly. The electrochemical performance was tested by assembling the active electrode into coin-type (CR2016) cells in an Ar-filled glovebox (MIKROUNA). SIBs were assembled with a sodium metal disk counter electrode. 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in a 1:1 volumetric mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and ethyl methyl carbonate (DEC) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate additive was used as the electrolyte. PIBs were assembled with potassium metal as the counter electrode and 0.8 M KPF<sub>6</sub> in a 1:1 volumetric mixture of EC and DEC as the electrolyte. The galvanostatic charge–discharge tests of SIBs and PIBs were carried out on a Neware battery testing system (CT-3008W) in voltage ranges of 0.01–3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and 0.01–1.5 V (vs K<sup>+</sup>/K), respectively. CV was conducted on the Chenhua electrochemical station.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c08950?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c08950</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM and TEM images for the Co<sub>5</sub>(O<sub>9.48</sub>H<sub>8.52</sub>)NO<sub>3</sub> NFs/CC precursor; EDX spectrum for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC; XRD pattern; Co 2p and P 2p XPS spectra for CoP powder; sodium storage performance of pure CC; charge–discharge curves for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NFs/CC at different rates; SEM images for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NSs/CC after different cycles; and sodium storage performance for Co(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> NFs/CC in comparison with previous reported metal phosphates/sulfides/oxides (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08950/suppl_file/am1c08950_si_001.pdf">am1c08950_si_001.pdf (925.82 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c08950" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhDFA5tnRorqA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201904320"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium/Potassium-Ion Batteries: Boosting The Rate Capability and Cycle Life by Combining Morphology, Defect and Structure Engineering</span>. <i>Adv. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>32</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1904320</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adma.201904320</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D32%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D1904320%26journal%3DAdv.%2BMater.%26author%3DH.%2BHuang%26author%3DR.%2BXu%26author%3DY.%2BFeng%26author%3DS.%2BZeng%26author%3DY.%2BJiang%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DW.%2BLuo%26author%3DY.%2BYu%26title%3DSodium%252FPotassium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BBoosting%2BThe%2BRate%2BCapability%2Band%2BCycle%2BLife%2Bby%2BCombining%2BMorphology%252C%2BDefect%2Band%2BStructure%2BEngineering%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadma.201904320&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c08950&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadma.201904320&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium/Potassium-Ion Batteries: Boosting the Rate Capability and Cycle Life by Combining Morphology, Defect and Structure Engineering</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Huang, Huijuan; Xu, Rui; Feng, Yuezhan; Zeng, Sifan; Jiang, Yu; Wang, Huijuan; Luo, Wei; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1904320</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon-based materials have been considered as the most promising anode materials for both sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs), owing to their good chem. stability, high elec. cond., and environmental benignity.  However, due to the large sizes of sodium and potassium ions, it is a great challenge to realize a carbon anode with high reversible capacity, long cycle life, and high rate capability.  Herein, by rational design, N-doped 3D mesoporous carbon nanosheets (N-CNS) are successfully synthesized, which can realize unprecedented electrochem. performance for both SIBs and PIBs.  The N-CNS possess an ultrathin nanosheet structure with hierarchical pores, ultrahigh level of pyridinic N/pyrrolic N, and an expanded interlayer distance.  The beneficial features that can enhance the Na-/K-ion intercalation/deintercalation kinetic process, shorten the diffusion length for both ions and electrons, and accommodate the vol. change are demonstrated.  Hence, the N-CNS-based electrode delivers a high capacity of 239 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1 after 10 000 cycles for SIBs and 321 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1 after 5000 cycles for PIBs.  First-principles calcn. shows that the ultrahigh doping level of pyridinic N/pyrrolic N contributes to the enhanced sodium and potassium storage performance by modulating the charge d. distribution on the carbon surface.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpVG-cQvHto_LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0linvP633bxqWg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXpt12kuw%253D%253D&amp;md5=03d6ec4c0f67558dd6c91962115a7d5f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/sus2.4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, X.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A perspective on sustainable energy materials for lithium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cheng, Xin-Bing; Liu, He; Yuan, Hong; Peng, Hong-Jie; Tang, Cheng; Huang, Jia-Qi; Zhang, Qiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">SusMat</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium ion battery has achieved great success in portable electronics and even recently electronic vehicles since its commercialization in 1990s.  However, lithium-ion batteries are confronted with several issues in terms of the sustainable development such as the high price of raw materials and electronic products, the emerging safety accidents, etc.  The recent progresses are herein emphasized on lithium batteries for energy storage to clearly understand the sustainable energy chem. and emerging energy materials.  The Perspective presents novel lithium-ion batteries developed with the aims of enhancing the electrochem. performance and sustainability of energy storage systems.  First, revolutionary material chemistries, including novel low-cobalt cathode, org. electrode, and aq. electrolyte, are discussed.  Then, the characteristics of safety performance are analyzed and strategies to enhance safety are subsequently evaluated.  Battery recycling is considered as the key factor for a sustainable society and related technologies are present as well.  Finally, conclusion and outlook are drawn to shed lights on the further development of sustainable lithium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqqi-VUKeoyALVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0linvP633bxqWg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3sXivFGis7c%253D&amp;md5=5e9381afd15f9f89e00b8a074fe86aed</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201903194"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mi, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Progress on The Alloy-Based Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries and Potassium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Small</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>17</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1903194</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent progress on alloy-based anode for sodium-ion batteries and potassium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Song, Keming; Liu, Chuntai; Mi, Liwei; Chou, Shulei; Chen, Weihua; Shen, Changyu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">17</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1903194</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMALBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1613-6810</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  High-energy batteries with low cost are urgently needed in the field of large-scale energy storage, such as grid systems and renewable energy sources.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) with alloy-based anodes provide huge potential due to their earth abundance, high capacity, and suitable working potential, and are recognized as attractive alternatives for next-generation batteries system.  Although some important breakthroughs have been reported, more significant improvements are still required for long lifetime and high energy d.  Herein, the latest progress for alloy-based anodes for SIBs and PIBs is summarized, mainly including Sn, Sb, Ge, Bi, Si, P, and their oxides, sulfides, selenides, and phosphides.  Specifically, the material designs for the desired Na+/K+ storage performance, phase transform, ionic/electronic transport kinetics, and specific chem. interactions are discussed.  Typical structural features and research strategies of alloy-based anodes, which are used to facilitate processes in battery development for SIBs and PIBs, are also summarized.  The perspective of future research of SIBs and PIBs is outlined.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrGUYAFdT5VMLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0linvP633bxqWg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvVenur7K&amp;md5=7fc1a6db75a43c3f8ea178a3920d5d21</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/sus2.v1.1"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Renewable Biomass-Derived Carbons for Electrochemical Capacitor Applications</span>. <i>SusMat</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">30</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Graphite anode for potassium-ion battery with unprecedented performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Ling; Ma, Ruifang; Zhang, Qingfeng; Jia, Xinxin; Lu, Bingan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">58</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite as an anode for the potassium ion battery (PIBs) has the merits of low cost and potentially high energy d., while suffering from limited cycle time and inferior stability.  Herein we, using a concd. electrolyte, demonstrate that formation of a robust inorg.-rich passivation layer on the graphite anode could resolve these problems.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrFEZ8wJYk1irVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgChL8vqda5Sw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXksVeqs7g%253D&amp;md5=e4bc2edac8d22121e7986e165b7df007</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/anie.202102954"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tunable Hollow Nanoreactors for <i>in situ</i> Synthesis of GeP Electrodes towards High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Angew.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tunable Hollow Nanoreactors for In Situ Synthesis of GeP Electrodes towards High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zeng, Tianbiao; He, Hanna; Guan, Huibin; Yuan, Ruoxin; Liu, Xingang; Zhang, Chuhong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The practical application of germanium phosphide (GeP) in battery systems is seriously impeded referring to the sluggish reaction kinetics and severe vol. change.  Nanostructure design that elaborately resolves the above issues is highly desired but still remains a big challenge.  Herein, unique hollow nanoreactors assembled with nitrogen-doped carbon networks for in situ synthesis of the GeP electrodes are proposed for the first time.  Such nanoreactors form a self-supported conductive network, ensuring sufficient electrolyte infiltration and fast electron transport.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anion Vacancies Regulating Endows MoSSe with Fast and Stable Potassium Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Hanna; Huang, Dan; Gan, Qingmeng; Hao, Junnan; Liu, Sailin; Wu, Zhibin; Pang, Wei Kong; Johannessen, Bernt; Tang, Yougen; Luo, Jing-Li; Wang, Haiyan; Guo, Zaiping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Vacancy engineering is a promising approach for optimizing the energy storage performance of transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) due to the unique properties of vacancies in manipulating the electronic structure and active sites.  Nevertheless, achieving effective introduction of anion vacancies with adjustable vacancy concn. on a large scale is still a big challenge.  Herein, MoS2(1-x)Se2x alloys with anion vacancies introduced in situ have been achieved by a simple alloying reaction, and the vacancy concn. has been optimized through adjusting the chem. compn.  Exptl. and d. functional theory calcn. results suggest that the anion vacancies in MoS2(1-x)Se2x alloys could enhance the electronic cond., induce more active sites, and alleviate structural variation in the alloys during the potassium storage process. Moreover, a reversible capacity of 220.5 mAh g-1 could be maintained at 2000 mA g-1 after 1000 cycles.  This work demonstrates a practical approach to modifying the electronic and defect properties of TMDs, providing an effective strategy for constructing advanced electrode materials for battery systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoXdcW5odM6NLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgChL8vqda5Sw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvVantrbK&amp;md5=bdf190a3483499180c6f2375a012a109</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/smll.202001265"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Flexible Film with SnS2 Nanoparticles Chemically Anchored on 3D-Graphene Framework for High Areal Density and High Rate Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sang, Zhiyuan; Yan, Xiao; Su, Dong; Ji, Huiming; Wang, Sihui; Dou, Shi Xue; Liang, Ji</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The design and construction of flexible electrodes that can function at high rates and high areal capacities are essential regarding the practical application of flexible sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and other energy storage devices, which remains significantly challenging by far.  Herein, a flexible and 3D porous graphene nanosheet/SnS2 (3D-GNS/SnS2) film is reported as a high-performance SIB electrode.  In this hybrid film, the GNS/SnS2 microblocks serve as pillars to assemble into a 3D porous and interconnected framework, enabling fast electron/ion transport; while the GNS bridges the GNS/SnS2 microblocks into a flexible framework to provide satisfactorily mech. strength and long-range cond.  Moreover, the SnS2 nanocrystals, which chem. bond with GNS, provide sufficient active sites for Na storage and ensure the cycling stability.  Consequently, this flexible 3D-GNS/SnS2 film exhibits excellent Na-storage performances, esp. in terms of high areal capacity (2.45 mAh cm-2) and high rates with superior stability (385 mAh g-1 at 1.0 A g-1 over 1000 cycles with ≈100% retention).  A flexible SIB full cell using this anode exhibits high and stable performance under various bending situations.  Thus, this work provide a feasible route to prep. flexible electrodes with high practical viability for not only SIBs but also other energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr2h2aEibZNJ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgChL8vqda5Sw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXpvFahsbw%253D&amp;md5=b79bf1a76101256ff85f809a573f8e44</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201908445"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sarkar, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Q.-A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Progress in Advanced Organic Electrode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Synthesis, Mechanisms, Challenges and Perspectives</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Progress in Advanced Organic Electrode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Synthesis, Mechanisms, Challenges and Perspectives</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yin, Xiuping; Sarkar, Samrat; Shi, Shanshan; Huang, Qiu-An; Zhao, Hongbin; Yan, Liuming; Zhao, Yufeng; Zhang, Jiujun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
